Path of Accumulation

Huangdi's Suwen

On the Generation of the Five Organs

The pulse of the Yellow Emperor is large and vacuous, with accumulated qi in the abdomen, and a constricting disease named "jue xian" occurs, similarly affecting women. It leads to illness in the limbs, with sweating occurring in the presence of wind.

[Note: The abdomen is the enclosure of the spleen and earth. When sweating occurs in the limbs due to wind, it indicates that wind-dampness has invaded the spleen, causing accumulated qi. This is because the evil of wind-wood internally dries the earth of the spleen, and the dampness interacts with the yin earth, thus retaining and accumulating. If the spleen qi cannot irrigate the surroundings, it reverses internally and leads to constricting qi.][1p]
Treatise on Qi Jue

When heat from the small intestine moves to the large intestine, it causes the condition of "ji" and results in heaviness.
[Note: "Ji" refers to a gathering that forms by accumulating fluids, as the small intestine governs liquids and the large intestine governs fluids. When heat from the small intestine transfers to the large intestine, it leads to the retention of fluids, resulting in "fu ji".]

Treatise on the Abdomen

The Emperor asked: There is fullness in the lower abdomen, with roots present on all sides—what illness is this? Is it treatable? Qi Bo replied: This illness is called "fu liang." The Emperor asked: How does one acquire "fu liang"? Qi Bo said: It is due to the presence of large amounts of pus and blood outside the intestines and stomach, making it untreatable. If one attempts to treat it, cutting and pressing can lead to death. The Emperor questioned: Why is this so? Qi Bo explained: If it descends, it will cause yin to manifest, leading to the downward flow of pus and blood; if it ascends, it will press upon the stomach and cause internal masses. This indicates a prolonged illness, making it difficult to treat. If it is above the navel, it is a reversal; if below the navel, it follows. One should not act hastily to expel it, as this relates to needling practices.
[Note: The roots present on all sides indicate that this illness resides in the blood, with channels connecting above and below. It is untreatable by massage; pressing for dissolution will surely cause pain and potentially death. This relates to a tangible pathology that is not easily resolved. "Below" refers to the lower abdomen; "yin" refers to the anterior and posterior yin. Thus, due to the presence of yin, downward flow of pus and blood is inevitable.][2p]
If one follows the channels upwards, it affects the stomach cavity and the area around the stomach, leading to the accumulation of pus and blood outside the intestines and stomach, resembling a large bag. Above the navel is the air division of the abdomen, hence it is opposite; below the navel is the blood division of the womb, which is easier to flow and thus aligned. One must not move this; it must not be massaged or disturbed, but should be welcomed and taken away immediately.

The Emperor asked: If a person’s body and thighs are swollen, and there is pain around the navel, what illness is this? Qi Bo replied: This condition is called "suppressed beam," which is the root of wind. Its energy overflows from the large intestine and settles in the lower abdomen. The original point of the lower abdomen is below the navel, hence the pain around the navel. It should not be moved; moving it would cause a condition of dampness related to water.

(Note: The movement of qi between the skin and muscle causes swelling in the body and limbs. This wind pathogen injures the qi and stays around the navel and abdomen; hence it is called the root of wind. The wind's energy overflows outside the large intestine and remains in the lower abdomen, exactly below the navel, causing pain around it. It should not be recklessly attacked to provoke the water; if the water overflows upwards, it will negatively affect urination.)[3p]
The Emperor said: “I suffer from fullness and reversed qi in my lower abdomen; it has persisted for two or three years. What is the cause of this illness?” Qi Bo replied: “The illness is called 'si ji' (breath accumulation). It does not obstruct eating, and it should not be treated with moxibustion or needling. The accumulation can be addressed with guiding and medication, but the medicine alone cannot cure it.”

(Note: The lungs govern qi and control breathing, hence lung accumulation is referred to as 'si ji'. It gradually builds up over time, which is why it has lasted two or three years. This illness has a tangible presence in the abdomen and cannot be treated with needling. One should use the methods of guiding and harmonizing medications; both approaches should be undertaken together for the illness to be cured. If only the medicine is used without guiding, it cannot be treated effectively.)

When the kidney pulse is slightly rapid, the liver pulse is slightly rapid, and the heart pulse is slightly rapid, but there is no swelling, all indicate 'jia' (a type of stagnation).

(Note: 'Slightly rapid' refers to a cold and deficient pulse. The viscera qi is retained; hence the pulse presents this way.) When the three yang pulses are rapid, it indicates stagnation.

(Note: Illness in the qi of the three yang leads to stagnation.)

In relation to the four seasons: If the jueyin channel is congested, it causes ailments of fullness in the lower abdomen; if the shaoyin channel is congested, it leads to blood accumulation in the urine; if the taiyin channel is congested, it results in fullness in the heart and abdomen; if the yangming channel is congested, it presents as accumulation along with frequent fright; if the taiyang channel is congested, it manifests as accumulation with frequent headaches; if the shaoyang channel is congested, it results in accumulation with frequent tightness in the muscles and eye pain.

(Note: Congestion refers to more blood and less qi, with slight cold present. Blood conditions are termed accumulations, due to excess blood that becomes stagnant and forms a mass.)

(From the Ling Shu - The Classic of the Spirit Pivot)

(Section on the manifestations of evil qi affecting the organs and their shapes)[4p]
The heart pulse is slightly slow, indicative of a retention beneath the heart, moving up and down, sometimes spitting blood. [Note: The retention beneath the heart is due to excess, hence the slightness. If evil energy is thin beneath the heart, and the heart governs blood, heat will overflow, hence the occasional spitting of blood.]

The spleen pulse is slightly large, presenting as great pus and blood in the abdomen, outside of the intestines and stomach. [Note: “Large” indicates excess. The great pus and blood in the abdomen signify excess accumulation.]

The liver pulse is slightly rapid, representing phlegm or fat, located beneath the ribs. If covered like a cup, it is slightly slow, representing water dysphoria. [Note: The liver is suffering from rapidity, hence the slightly rapid pulse indicates fat or phlegm. If beneath the ribs resembles being covered, it indicates excess energy. Water dysphoria is also due to accumulation from food and drink.]

The lung pulse is slippery and strong, indicating respiration and upward qi. [Note: Slippery indicates abundant yang energy, hence the indication of respiratory upward qi.]

Huang Di asked: How can one recognize the accumulation of disorders in the intestines? Shao Yu replied: The skin is thin and lacks moisture, the flesh is soft and not firm; in this way, the intestines and stomach become ill. When ill, evil energies linger and accumulate, damaging the connection between the spleen and stomach. If cold and warmth are not taken in turns, and evil energy slightly arrives, accumulations from grains and livestock will remain, leading to a major accumulation. [Note: Fluids, blood, and qi are generated by the intestines and stomach. When they become ill, evil qi lingers and forms accumulations, damaging the relationship between spleen and stomach. If food and drink are further consumed without regard to temperature, evil energy that arrives will accumulate, leading to major gathering.][5p]
The Internal Organs Chapter

When the liver is elevated, it affects the upper diaphragm, causing the sides of the ribs to react in breathing.

Note: The liver meridian connects to the diaphragm and extends to the lungs, hence the effect on breathing and rib discomfort.

The Water Distension Chapter

The Yellow Emperor asked: What is the condition of the intestines? Qi Bo replied: Cold qi is lodged outside the intestines, clashing with the defensive qi, preventing the qi from nourishing, which causes it to become obstructed and accumulate internally, leading to the rise of evil qi and the formation of polyps. At the beginning, they are the size of a chicken egg, gradually growing larger until they resemble a child in the womb. If they persist, they may detach from the organs; when pressed, they feel firm, and when pushed, they can move. Menstrual issues occur timely; this is its indication.

Note: Cold qi outside the intestines clashes with the defensive qi, preventing it from nourishing properly. The obstruction happens due to intangible qi coming into conflict in the empty spaces outside the intestines, accumulating on the tangible membranes. Thus, the evil qi of blood and flesh arises, leading to the formation of these growths.[6p]
For a long time, it can separate from the membranes of the organs, like a fetus suspended in the womb, firm when pressed and movable when pushed, not involving the organs. Therefore, the monthly cycle occurs regularly; this is its sign. Huangdi asked: "What about stone mass (shijia)?" Qibo replied: "Stone mass forms within the womb, where cold qi lingers at the entrance (zimen). The entrance is blocked, causing qi not to circulate, and the stagnant bad blood that should be expelled is retained, growing larger day by day, resembling a fetus. The monthly cycle does not occur regularly, all of this happens in women, and it can be guided downwards."

(Note: Cold qi lingering at zimen leads to blockage, causing blood in the womb to stagnate instead of being expelled, forming masses that grow larger over time, resembling a fetus. Blood remains in the womb, hence the monthly cycle does not occur. These conditions all arise in women, and treatment can guide it downwards.)[7p]
The Beginning of All Illnesses

The Yellow Emperor asked Qi Bo, saying: All illnesses begin with the changes of wind, rain, cold, heat, and with emotions such as joy and anger. When joy and anger are unregulated, they harm the internal organs; wind and rain harm the upper body; clear and damp conditions harm the lower body. The ailments caused by these three types differ, and I wish to learn about their connections. Qi Bo replied: The three types of energies are distinct. Some arise from yin, while others arise from yang; allow me to explain further! Unregulated joy and anger harm the internal organs; when the organs are harmed, illnesses arise from yin. When clear and damp conditions invade the weak, ailments arise from the lower body, and when wind and rain invade the weak, ailments arise from the upper body. This is what is meant by the three types. As for their overwhelming presence, it cannot be counted.

The Yellow Emperor said: I indeed cannot enumerate them, which is why I ask the Sage. I wish to fully understand this path! Qi Bo said: Wind, rain, cold, and heat cannot independently harm a person without a state of deficiency. If one suddenly encounters strong winds or violent rain and does not fall ill, it is likely because there is no deficiency. Therefore, the evil cannot harm independently. This must be due to the deficiency of the external wind interacting with the body's state of deficiency, leading to an affliction upon the body. When two strong forces meet, the flesh of most people remains firm.[8p]
Among the virtual evils, they rely on the timing of heaven, the physical form, and the interplay of emptiness and substance, leading to significant illness. There are defined spaces for Qi, categorized based on location, with divisions among the outer, inner, and middle realms.

[Note: The "virtual wind" refers to the spring winds from the west and summer winds from the north, energies from the five elements that are not balanced, thus harming the body. If a person's Qi is not deficient, even if faced with virtual winds, they cannot be harmed. Thus, it's necessary that both the individual and the heavenly conditions are deficient, causing interaction of both deficiencies, leading to a weakening of the form, unlike the common people's strong flesh. The three divisions refer to the following: the virtual evil impacting people, the external causes of illness; the aggregation that has already developed, the internal causes; the emotional and desire-related injuries to the organs, the ailments residing in the Yin. These represent the three aspects of internal and external.]

Therefore, the influence of virtual evils on individuals begins at the skin. When the skin is relaxed, the pores open, allowing evil to enter through the hair. Once it enters deeply, the hair stands up, indicating discomfort, and thus the skin experiences pain. If it lingers and does not leave, it affects the vessels and channels. At the channel level, pain manifests in the muscles; when pain occurs, breathing becomes labored, and the major meridians are altered. If it remains, it affects the meridians. At the meridian level, there are symptoms of chills and sudden fright. If it persists, it impacts the channels. At the channel level, the six major meridians become blocked, leading to joint pain in the limbs, and stiffness in the lower back. If it continues to persist, it settles in the pulse of the Chong and Yin.[9p]
During the time of伏冲 (Fu Chong), there is a heaviness and pain in the body. It lingers and does not leave, settling in the intestines and stomach. When in the intestines and stomach, there are sounds and bloating; if cold, there are rumblings and diarrhea, causing food to not digest; if hot, the result is a mucous-like discharge. If it lingers and does not leave, it transmits and settles outside the intestines and stomach, gathering in the vessels, stuck and not leaving, resting and forming a mass. It may affect the Sun meridian, or the collateral vessels, or the regular channels, or the Shu vessels, or the channels of伏冲, or affect the sinews, or abide in the intestines and stomach, reaching up to the ren vessels. The evil energy is pervasive and cannot be easily described.

(Note: The evil that invades the body must enter from the exterior. It starts at the skin; when a person's form is weak, the skin becomes lax, allowing the evil to take hold, causing the hair to stand on end, and blood and energy to stagnate, resulting in pain in the skin. Inspection of which channel is involved, whether deep or shallow, allows treatment from the exterior. If it lingers and does not leave, its entry becomes gradually deeper, hence it settles in the collateral vessels, causing pain between the muscles. If the muscle pain intermittently ceases, the evil will exit the collaterals and penetrate deeper, and the main channels will take on this condition; the evil energy enters the channels from the collaterals, still present in the exterior, hence causing chills and evil cold. However, the channel energy connects to the organs, so there is also a tendency to be startled.)[10p]
The areas of the defeated are where the vital energies gather, and their lingering must be between the joints and valleys. Therefore, if the pathogenic factor remains in the defeated, the Six Meridians become obstructed, leading to pain and stiffness in the limbs, back, and waist. The "Fuchong" refers to the channel of counterflow within the abdomen. When the pathogen remains inside, the blood and vital energies cannot fill the body, resulting in heaviness and pain. If it lingers and does not depart, it transmits to the intestines and stomach, causing symptoms like abdominal distension and noises; too much cold leads to rumbling and diarrhea, while too much heat results in loose stools. Outside the intestines and stomach is the area known as "Muyan," which refers to the outside of the skin and membranes. If it is detained there and does not leave, it stops and becomes a mass, similar to conditions like malaria and distension. The "Sun" and "Luo" channels are minor collateral channels within the "Muyan"; the meridians are the main channels of the stomach and intestines. The "Shu" channels are the major collaterals of the visceral organs, responsible for transporting the blood and vital energies of food and drink. The "Fuchong" flows within the abdominal channel. "Muyan" is akin to the acupoints "Zhongfu" as the meeting point and "Taiyuan" as the origin, and similar types. The "Lujin" are the muscles attached to the spine; the "Huanjin" are muscles circulating within the abdomen. These various factors are situated around, before, and after the intestines and stomach. Pathogenic factors accompany and cause accumulation, thus leading to numerous excesses. Huangdi said: "I wish to hear more about the origins of this!" Qibo replied: "The accumulations resulting from obstructions in the Sun collateral channels are those that move up and down. The arm is the residence of the hand's Sun collateral, which is floating and slack, thus unable to hold the accumulations in check."[11p]
Therefore, the movement and flow within the stomach and intestines, as water seeps and pours in, is accompanied by a sound like bubbling. When there is cold, it is like thunder rumbling, causing sharp pain at times. When it affects the Yangming channel, it resides around the navel; when full from eating, it swells, and when hungry, it shrinks. When it relates to the relaxing of the muscles, it resembles the accumulation in Yangming; fullness causes pain, while hunger brings relief. When it pertains to the root of the stomach and intestines, pain radiates to the relaxing muscles; when full, there is relief, and hunger brings pain. When it concerns the penetrating vessel, it can be felt by palpation and moves upon touch, generating heat and causing qi to descend to the thighs, resembling the condition of being poured over by broth. When it involves the muscles behind the intestines, hunger reveals the blockages, while fullness hides them, and pressing does not yield any response. When it pertains to the pathways of the channels, they are blocked and stagnant, preventing fluids from descending, causing the openings and orifices to be obstructed. This is how pathogenic qi enters from outside and affects the interior from above and below.

(Note: This commentary on internal conditions focuses on the ability of accumulations to shift up and down within the intestines. Accumulations in the networks of the large and small intestines belong to the channels, wherein the networks are superficial and relaxed, unable to contain the accumulation and stop it, thus causing mobility within the stomach and intestines. If there is water, it seeps outward, creating a bubbling sound. If there is cold, it leads to bloating and rumbling akin to thunder, with associated sharp pain at times.)[12p]
The channels of the Yangming are the great connecting vessels of the stomach. Therefore, its accumulation is situated near the navel. When filled, the fluids of food and water pour outward, hence in great hunger, the fluids and blood are insufficient, leading to a smaller condition. The tension of the muscles between the sinews appears like the accumulation of Yangming. When full, it causes distension and pain; when hungry, the energy recedes and becomes stable. The membranes of the intestines and stomach are the origin of pain, connected through the tension of the muscles. When full, the fluids seep outward, providing comfort; when hungry, it becomes dry and painful. The channel of the Fu-Chong lies between the navel, hence it responds to the hand and moves. When the hand feels warmth, it indicates that the blood and energy of the Chong channel are abundant outward. Those resembling the state of being poured out are caused by accumulation and subsequently generate heat. The lumbar sinews are attached within the spine, behind the intestines; thus, when hungry, the intestines appear empty and visible, while when full, they are not visible and cannot be pressed. The transportation channels are meant to connect blood and energy; if blocked, then the fluids do not descend, causing the pores of the skin and hair to dry up.

The Yellow Emperor asked: When the accumulation first arises until it is completed, what should be done? Qi Bo replied: When the accumulation first arises, it is caused by cold, and the stagnation leads to accumulation. The Yellow Emperor asked: How does it become fully accumulated? Qi Bo replied: The stagnation engenders cold in the feet, which causes the blood vessels to solidify. When the blood vessels solidify, the cold energy enters the intestines and stomach; once in the intestines and stomach, it causes bloating. The bloating then causes the fluids outside the intestines to gather and cannot disperse, accumulating day by day.[13p]
Suddenly overeating or drinking leads to a full intestine; irregular living and excessive exertion harm the meridians. If the yang meridians are damaged, blood leaks externally, resulting in nosebleeds; if the yin meridians are harmed, blood leaks internally, causing rectal bleeding. When the meridians of the intestines and stomach are injured, blood spills outside the intestines, mixing with cold fluids, which congeal with the blood, preventing dispersion and accumulating over time.

Suddenly exposed to cold externally, or suffering internal damage from worry and anger leads to reversed qi, causing obstruction of the six yang channels, preventing the flow of dampness, and coagulating blood, which then accumulates. Body fluids drip and do not disperse, leading to congestion.

[Note: Cold reverses downwards, causing pain and obstruction in the limbs, making movement inconvenient. When coldness enters through the legs, blood and qi congeal; this cold rises into the intestines and stomach, causing them to become cold, preventing yang qi from transforming and leading to distension. This distension results in fluids outside the intestines that accumulate and do not disperse over time. Sudden overeating or drinking may exceed the intestines' capacity for processing, causing fluids to spill outside the membranes, mixing with blood, leading to food accumulation, similar to conditions in infants. Irregular living and excessive exertion may also harm the meridians. Damage to the upward-moving yang meridians causes blood to leak externally above, resulting in nosebleeds, whereas damage to the downward-moving yin meridians leads to internal bleeding below.][14p]
When the channels of the stomach and intestines are injured, blood spills outside the intestines. If there is cold fluid and foam outside the intestines that clashes with the blood, it combines and凝聚成. Sometimes, sudden cold evil from outside, combined with internal injuries from worry and anger, causes qi to reverse upward. When qi reverses upward, the flow through the six channels becomes obstructed, the warming and nourishing energy fails to circulate, blood congeals and accumulates, and fluids become thick within the channels, seeping outside without dispersing and accumulating.

The Yellow Emperor asked: What about those conditions stemming from yin? Qibo replied: Worry and contemplation injure the heart; heavy cold injures the lungs; anger injures the liver; drunkenness in bed, with sweat under the wind, injures the spleen; excessive exertion, especially after sweat during intimacy, injures the kidneys. These are the illnesses born from both internal and external factors.

Note: Injury to the heart affects yang, injury to the lungs affects qi, injury to the liver affects blood, injury to the spleen affects the nourishing and protective aspect, and injury to the kidneys affects true yin. Any injury to the organs represents an illness born from yin.

The Yellow Emperor said: Good. How should one treat this? Qibo answered: Observe where it hurts to understand its response; if there is excess or deficiency, supplement what is deficient and drain what is excessive, without going against the seasonal changes. This is called the ultimate treatment.

Note: Observing where it hurts and understanding its response is about accumulating knowledge of the five organs. By examining the left and right, upward and downward, one can correspond with the acupuncture points of the five organ channels, discerning excess and deficiency for treatment, without going against the seasonal changes. This is called the ultimate treatment.[15p]
Bian Que's Difficult Classics

Division of the Five Organs

Illness can manifest as accumulation or aggregation; how can we distinguish between them? Accumulation pertains to yin qi, while aggregation pertains to yang qi. Therefore, accumulation is characterized by being deep and dormant, while aggregation is characterized by being superficial and active. What is called accumulation is named for where qi collects, and what is called aggregation is named for where qi congregates. Accumulation arises from the five organs, while aggregation is formed by the six fu organs. Accumulation, being yin qi, has a constant location for onset, with pain confined to its area, having an origin and limit both above and below, as well as side to side. Aggregation, being yang qi, has no fixed place for onset, with pain not confined to a specific area, thus called aggregation. Hence, this is how we differentiate between accumulation and aggregation.

The accumulation of the liver is called "Fat Qi," located beneath the left ribs like a cup, having both a head and feet. If it does not heal for a long time, it may cause cough, fever, and malaria, lasting for years without relief.

The accumulation of the heart is called "Hidden Beam," arising above the navel, as large as an arm, extending to beneath the heart. If it does not heal for a long time, it may lead to worrying and vexing the heart.

The accumulation of the spleen is known as "Fullness Qi," situated in the stomach region, as large as a plate. If it does not heal for a long time, it may cause the limbs to wither, lead to jaundice, and prevent food from nourishing the skin.

The accumulation of the lungs is called "Breath Distress," located beneath the right ribs, as large as a cup. If it lingers, it may cause chills and fever, cough, and lead to lung abscesses.

The accumulation of the kidneys is called "Swollen Essence," originating in the lower abdomen and reaching beneath the heart, resembling a tumescent fish, moving up and down without a certain time. If it persists, it may lead to coughing, breathlessness, brittle bones, and shortness of breath.[16p]
Essential Prescriptions from the Golden Coffer (Han Dynasty - Zhang Ji)

On Diseases, Pulses, Symptoms, and Treatments

A question is asked: What do the terms accumulation, gathering, and (殸禾) qi refer to in diseases? The teacher replies: Accumulation refers to diseases of the organs which do not shift; gathering refers to diseases of the bowels which manifest at times, moving and shifting, making them treatable. (殸禾) qi refers to pain below the ribs that, when pressed, alleviates, but may recur as (殸禾) qi. The primary signs of accumulation are seen when the pulse is thin and adheres to the bone. The cun position indicates accumulation in the chest; when slightly protruding from the cun, accumulation is in the throat; at the guan position, it indicates accumulation near the navel; at the upper guan position, it is below the heart; slightly below the guan position, accumulation is in the lower abdomen; at the chi position, it is in the qi rushing area. If the pulse comes from the left, the accumulation is on the left; if it comes from the right, the accumulation is on the right; if it is bilateral, the accumulation is central. Each is treated according to its respective location.[17p]
The Book of Accumulation and Aggregation by Hua Tuo (Han Dynasty)

Accumulation, aggregation, symptoms, and parasites arise when the true qi of the five organs and six bowels is lost and邪气 (evil qi) invades. Over time, if not removed, they can manifest as accumulations or aggregates, symptoms or lumps, and can transform or act as parasites, each presenting differently. Some can harm people, while others cannot. Some are slow to cause illness, while others act quickly. Some cause pain, while others cause itching. Some arise in the head or limbs, while others have a solid form. Their manifestations vary widely.

This is a result of mutual influences between the internal and external, the true and the evil, where qi and blood conflict and combine. Accumulations are connected to the organs, aggregates to the bowels, symptoms to qi, lumps to blood, and parasites arise from the interactions of blood, qi, and food. There are five types of accumulations, six types of aggregates, twelve types of symptoms, eight types of lumps, and nine types of parasites, with names that vary.

The five accumulations are named after the heart, liver, spleen, lungs, and kidneys; the six aggregates are named after the large intestine, small intestine, gallbladder, stomach, bladder, and triple warmer; the twelve symptoms include labor, qi, cold, heat, deficiency, excess, wind, dampness, food, medicine, thought, and worry; the eight lumps include blue, yellow, dryness, blood, fat, fox, snake, and turtle; the nine parasites are named as hookworm, roundworm, white worm, flesh-eating worm, lung worm, stomach worm, red worm, weak worm, and pinworm.[18p]
Thousand Gold Prescriptions
By Sun Simiao of the Tang Dynasty

Distinguishing Between Accumulation and Gathering

Disease can be either accumulation or gathering; how can we differentiate between them? The answer is: accumulation is yin qi, whereas gathering is yang qi. Thus, accumulation is heavy and dormant, while gathering is light and dynamic. What is stored as accumulation is termed "accumulation," and what is concentrated as gathering is termed "gathering." Therefore, accumulation is generated by the five viscera, while gathering is formed by the six intestines. Accumulation, being yin qi, has a constant place of origin, its pain remains localized, and it has clear beginnings and ends above and below, as well as defined limits to the left and right. Gathering, being yang qi, has no fixed origin, it does not stop above and below, and its pain lacks a specific location, hence it is referred to as gathering. Thus, this is how we differentiate between accumulation and gathering.[19p]
On Treating Accumulations

There are five accumulations and six gatherings. Accumulations arise from the yin energy of the five organs; gatherings are formed from the yang energy of the six intestines. This occurs due to the disharmony of yin and yang, the weakness of the organs, and the invasion of pathogenic wind, leading to the formation of accumulations and gatherings.

Just like the emotions of worry, gratitude, joy, and anger, which are inescapable for humans, excess can harm the five organs, disrupt the four seasons, and create blockages, which then solidify into the five accumulations. Thus, in the liver, it is called "fatty qi," in the heart it is "hidden beam," in the spleen it is "stomach distension," in the lungs it is "breath disturbance," and in the kidneys it is "running piglet." Though the names differ, the symptoms are also distinct.

The manifestation of "fatty qi" is observed under the left rib, covering a large area like a cup, being large and bulbous, resembling a head and feet, indicating liver accumulation. The pulse is thin and wiry, the complexion is blue, and symptoms include pain in the sides, pulling in the lower abdomen, cold feet, muscle cramps, with males presenting as hernia and females as masses.

The manifestation of "hidden beam" starts below the navel and is as large as an arm, rising to beneath the heart, resembling a beam across the chest diaphragm, indicating heart accumulation. The pulse is deep and hollow, the complexion is red, and symptoms include abdominal heat, red face, dry throat, heart vexation, and in severe cases, vomiting blood, leading to reduced appetite and weight loss.

The manifestation of "stomach distension" is situated in the epigastrium, covering a large area like a cup, being obstructed and unyielding, indicating spleen accumulation. The pulse is floating, large, and long, the complexion is yellow, and symptoms include decreased appetite when hungry, increased discomfort when full, abdominal fullness, vomiting, diarrhea, swollen feet, and muscle wasting, persisting for a long time without improvement.[20p]
The condition known as “Xi Ben” appears beneath the right ribs, large like a cup, with labored breathing and overflowing signs; this is called lung accumulation. Upon examining the pulse, it is floating and feeble, the complexion is pale, and the disease manifests as rebellious qi, back pain, shortness of breath, a tendency to forgetfulness, closed eyes, cold skin, and intermittent pain within the flesh, sometimes like the sensation of lice crawling or like the prick of a needle.

The condition known as “Ben Tun” arises from the lower abdomen, rising up to the heart area, occurring intermittently, and resembling a pig running; this is called kidney accumulation. Examining the pulse reveals it to be deep and urgent, the complexion is dark, and the symptoms are evident when hungry, reducing when full; there is urgency in the lower abdomen, lower back pain, dry mouth, blurred vision, cold bones, and prolonged illness leads to bone atrophy and shortness of breath.

Furthermore, when examining the six accumulations in the six bowels, the condition is different. How so? The six bowels belong to the three Yangs; Tai Yang expels clear qi, Yang Ming releases turbid qi, and Shao Yang transforms essential qi, functioning like the capital within a city, responsible for the regular transport and transformation of qi. If the six bowels become unbalanced, evil qi accumulates and does not disperse, starting with no fundamental root, unstopping above and below, thus pain lacks a constant location; if pain occurs above, it feels constrained, and below, it feels distended, with pain on both sides as if there were a solid mass that is easily rotated, hence it is different from the five accumulations.

In general, when examining pulses, a tight and surging pulse indicates accumulation, a floating yet firm pulse indicates accumulation, a horizontal pulse indicates accumulation beneath the ribs, and a small, deep, firm pulse indicates accumulation in the stomach. Overall, each illness presents its signs, and treatments have their respective methods. For example, in diagnosing abdominal accumulations, a firm, strong, urgent pulse indicates life, while a weak, urgent pulse indicates death. Similarly, strong and firm pulses point towards life, while weak, small pulses indicate death. This should not be overlooked.[21p]
Summary of Diagnosis and Treatment

Symptoms refer to hardness in the abdomen that is palpable when pressed. "The Sacred Remedy" states: Symptoms are akin to signs. Water should be soft and yielding, yet now it is hard like the earth; excess leads to harm, so one must contain it. Therefore, excessive dampness causes illnesses like spasms, which also involve the influence of wind. An excess of wind illness leads to dryness, causing the tendons and vessels to become tense, which also includes the influence of metal. An excess of dryness results in irritability and thirst, coupled with the influence of fire. Excessive heat can lead to the expulsion of bodily fluids, resulting in shivering and cold, which also involves the influence of water.

In treatment, one should simply purge the excessive pathogenic energy as the root of the illness, without mistakenly treating the accompanying transformations.[22p]
However, those who integrate transformations are following the way of heaven’s mechanisms and transformations, which, though present in the faint and nebulous, embody natural principles that are not manifest but have substance. Illness, though it arises from imbalance, is within the pursuit of transformation. The principles of the five elements can extend to the extent that they can also restrain each other, leading to the cessation of transformation.

When the wind and wood are vigorous and there is much wind, a strong wind will cause the temperature to drop—this is the wood being restrained by metal. Under intense cold, the air will become warmer—this is fire being supported by metal. When the summer heat reaches its peak, its essence will release moisture—this is fire being restrained by water. Consequently, the humid steam leads to clouds and rain, as earth transforms from water. When rain becomes excessive and is accompanied by fierce winds, wood restrains the earth.

Amidst the swirling winds, the autumn wind brings a chill, as metal is supported by wood. Once the chill reaches its extreme, all things become dry, as fire restrains metal. Consequently, winter cold is produced, as water transforms from fire. When the cold becomes extreme, water solidifies like earth, as earth restrains water.[23p]
When the ice and frost freeze completely, the eastern wind arises, transforming wood to support the earth, completing the cycle of the year. Those who do not understand the symptoms and roots of illness do so because they are unaware of this principle of change.

A lump (瘕) may feel hard in the abdomen, but it can suddenly gather or disperse, lacking a constant measure. Thus, the "Holy Prescriptions" states: a lump is akin to a falsehood, as its condition has not yet developed into a definitive ailment. The classical interpretation mentions: when blood does not flow and cold thins it, the blood remains stagnant within, forming a lump. Another explanation posits that it is a condition of accumulation in the abdomen. The classics say that heat from the small intestine moves to the large intestine and causes the condition known as "lump" or "sinking." The commentary notes that when the heat from the small intestine has already shifted into the large intestine, the two heats clash, resulting in blood overflow and forming a concealed lump. If the blood is stagnant and does not flow, it causes menstruation to be obstructed and not proceed, hence the term "lump" is associated with "sinking." "Lump" and "concealment" are synonymous. The lump can also refer to a hernia, a transcription error. Therefore, the classical texts indicate that there can also be heat associated with the condition of a lump.[24p]
When yang energy is stagnant, with heat accumulating and hardening without dissipating, it is not a cold syndrome, and should be differentiated by pulse and symptoms. The transfer of heat from the greater yang affecting the small intestine is called "excessive accumulation," where the fluids are depleted and cannot smooth and facilitate the bowels, leading to stagnation in the large intestine, which is managed by areca nut.

In cases of fullness below the ribs, with persistent counterflow of qi, where qi accumulates below the ribs, it breathes but does not dissipate, accumulating without dispersing, and the vital energy is in the stomach, obstructing food and drink, needling is not appropriate; it is better to use guiding methods and medication, with Baizhu pills prescribed.

Water loses its ability to disperse when dry and cannot dissipate when moist, resulting in fluid accumulation. Transmission of disease to the weaker organ can lead to death, whereas disease that does not transfer can be treated. For example, if lung disease transmits to the liver, and liver disease affects the spleen, spleen disease influences the kidneys, kidney disease impacts the heart, and heart disease transmits back to the lungs, each is influenced by the weaker organ.

When the qi of the five organs is deficient, and various external evils invade, it results in stagnation and accumulation. The pulse felt will be deep, thin, and weak.[25p]
Ruzhong's Filial Duty (by Zhang Congzheng)

The Treatment of the Same and the Melancholic

The sages have spoken clearly about the five accumulations and six conglomerations, yet the treatment methods remain obscure. They explain the five accumulations as follows: The accumulation of the liver is called "fatty qi," located under the left rib, resembling an overturned cup; it has heads and feet. If it lasts for a long time, it may cause coughing, reverse fever, and intermittent malaria. For those who suffer from this for years, it is the result. The accumulation of the heart is known as "the hidden beam," arising from the navel, as large as an arm, extending upward to below the heart. If it persists for a long time, it may lead to restlessness and mental distress. The accumulation of the spleen is referred to as "spleen qi," located in the stomach area, covering like a large dish. If it lasts for long, it may result in limbs becoming withdrawn and jaundice appearing, causing food not to nourish the skin, popularly known as "food labor yellow." The accumulation of the lungs is called "breath obstruction," situated under the left rib and covering like a cup. If it does not heal for long, it can lead to chills, fever, coughing, or lung abscesses. The accumulation of the kidneys is termed "turtle obstruction," originating in the lower abdomen and extending upward to below the heart, resembling a turtle in shape; it may fluctuate between upward and downward without a set time. If it persists for a long time, it can cause breathlessness, backward chi, bone atrophy, and shortness of breath.[26p]
The conditions of these five accumulations, as stated by the sages of old, are clear indeed. Upon visiting various medical practitioners, everyone can articulate this, and when asked about treatment methods, they merely mention substances like three-edged needles, guo herbs, lacquer, ochre, dried tangerine peel, mung stone, and castor beans. There are also those who, lacking clear differentiation between root and branch, attempt to supplement this understanding. Is it possible for a person suffering from accumulation, with major evil not having been expelled, to remedy it? As for the medicines commercially available for accumulation, I, when I was a novice in medicine, if I had ever used them, found them ineffective, and thus chose to change my approach. By studying the "Inner Canon," I suddenly had a profound realization. The "Inner Canon" states: Wood congestion leads to vomiting, Fire congestion results in sweating, Earth congestion causes purging, Metal congestion leads to urination, and Water congestion causes reversal. Wang Taifu explained: “Vomit” refers to expelling through the mouth; “sweat” refers to perspiration; “purge” refers to bowel movements; “urinate” refers to relieving the bladder; “reversal” refers to countering the obstruction. These five methods are governed by the five movements, hence these five treatment strategies are established, seemingly in alignment with the five accumulations.[27p]
The five accumulations arise when one is affected by the pathogenic influences that defeat oneself and then passes this onto those one has defeated. During times of abundance, these influences are resisted and not accepted, returning instead to the ones that defeat oneself. The ones that defeat oneself refuse to accept this, thus leading to the formation of an accumulation.

Therefore, the accumulation of the liver occurs on the day of Ji in late summer, the accumulation of the heart occurs on the days of Geng and Xin in autumn, the accumulation of the spleen occurs on the days of Ren and Gui in winter, the accumulation of the lungs occurs on the days of Jia and Yi in spring, and the accumulation of the kidneys occurs on the days of Bing and Ding in summer. All of these are due to oppression and withholding of the vital energy that is affected by pathogenic factors. Is it necessary to wait for the celestial authorities to intervene before these accumulations occur?

Moreover, the formation of these accumulations may be caused by aggressive emotions such as anger and joy or by sadness and fear; it may also be influenced by the consumption of certain foods that are sour, bitter, sweet, spicy, or salty, or by the intake of drinks that are warm, cool, hot, cold, or damp. Initial symptoms are often very slight, and breathing techniques or gentle pressure can often disperse them. Unfortunately, if one encounters an inept doctor who forcefully reinforces the condition, it will remain and eventually lead to the five accumulations. The essence of the fatty qi is not only an excess of qi but also involves blood, due to the liver's role in storing blood.[28p]
The "Fu Liang" condition refers to a build-up of heat, which, when treated with hot medicine, worsens, while cauterization causes it to accumulate further. Moreover, there are two types of "Fu Liang," which share the same name but differ in essence, requiring detailed explanation.

The first type has roots in all directions—up, down, left, and right—existing outside the intestines and stomach, accompanied by significant pus and blood. This type is similar to an abdominal abscess.

The second type presents swelling in the body, especially around the hips, thighs, and knees, with pain surrounding the navel. This is due to the root of wind and must not be moved; if moved, it can lead to water retention and difficulties with urination. The "Inner Canon" states that while these conditions should not be agitated, it refers to avoiding major purging rather than complete avoidance of treatment, as doing so may cause harm when the illness subsides.

Regarding "Pi Qi," it is commonly stated that cold leads to stagnation, but the "Inner Canon" considers dampness to be the culprit. Even if it arises from consuming cold drinks, the yang energy is trapped by the dampness. If attacked with heat, it does not disperse, but if with cold, the dampness leaves while the cold retreats.

As for "Xi Ben," it refers to the breath becoming short and rising upwards, which is an old interpretation. I believe "Ben" pertains to the gate known as "Ben Men," associated with the Hand Taiyin meridian. This connects the chest and penetrates the diaphragm, extending to the lower ribs. The symptoms include muscle pain, and in severe cases, this can lead to "Xi Ben."[29p]
The palm of the hand is associated with the arm, and its ailment manifests as chest pain and breathing difficulties. Additionally, if the lungs are low, it presses on the liver, causing pain in the sides. If the liver is high, it affects the upper region, leading to lateral discomfort. If we speak of this, it indicates stagnant energy in the chest that does not disperse. The "Spiritual Pivot" mentions that this kind of respiratory issue is frequent in relation to the five organs; hence I bring it up. The term "chest distress," akin to "chest heaving," is noted in "The Copper Man," which suggests it may arise from excessive reading, though this isn’t universally applicable. The kidneys govern the bones, and this accumulation is the most profound and difficult to treat. It is crucial to avoid inducing vomiting. Since it is located below, it is best to expel it from below. Therefore, I have previously used "Dusheng San" to expel damp energy, combined with a wooden frame, ensuring it is in a warm room to facilitate both vomiting and sweating. The accumulation in the liver is said to relate to wind. After expelling several liters, one must expect a drop or two of blood; do not doubt this as it is expected in illness. Follow with herbs that direct and regulate the accumulation, having treated concealed phlegm first using tea to adjust and induce vomiting simultaneously with sweating, and then use herbs to lower internal heat.[30p]
I have also treated issues of stagnation with great success. First, use the gourd stem powder to expel the sour, bitter, yellow, sticky, and rotten substances—about three to two sheng. Next, direct water using the methods of Yu to flow down two or three dozen times. Finally, mix in lighter herbal prescriptions like the Wu Ling San. I have also treated hiccups using the gourd stem powder, regardless of the season, placing it in a warm room, with an additional fire to assist sweating, and employing methods of inducing sweat and purging simultaneously. This condition should not be taken lightly; prolonged treatment can harm a person. I have also treated phlegm buildup by directing water to clear the channels, achieving results with one purge every three days, ten times in a month, totaling a hundred sessions over time. After that, I use active blood-moving and qi-transforming herbs to grind away the accumulation. Although this accumulation does not harm the body, it can lead to a shortened lifespan. For substances in the six accumulations, located in the organs and associated with yang, they are formless and lack a fixed treatment method; treating them in this manner is not difficult. Some say my treatment for accumulation is too severe. I respond, it is not so. Accumulation in the organs, like sediment in rivers, comes from multiple layers of fat membranes in convoluted passages, within the complex anatomy.[31p]
In the rivers and lakes, sediment is not found in the middle flow but often in the shallow bays and bends; when encountering overflow, it is swept away in a drift. Likewise, when it accumulates in the internal organs, the principle is the same. Therefore, I first use pills to eliminate recently ingested food, ensuring there are no blockages, leaving the broken-down remnants still displaced but not yet expelled; next, I use powdered medicine to fill the stomach and push everything down, like a raft navigating the river, clearing everything at once. If anything remains, I adjust with medicine. Only hard accumulations should not be treated this way and should be gradually removed. The "Inner Canon" states: for hard accumulations, one must cut them away. This is what people nowadays refer to as mass disorders.

For food accumulation: sourness in the stomach and fullness, herbs such as rhubarb and cowbane, worse cases involve muconate and croton. For wine accumulation: yellowing eyes and dry mouth, herbs like pueraria and wheat bran, serious cases include euphorbia and cowbane. For qi accumulation: belching and a feeling of oppression, herbs like aloes and areca nut, severe cases include bitter orange and cowbane. For phlegm accumulation: a throat feeling like it is being sawed, minerals like cinnabar and talc, in more severe cases, gourd seeds and euphorbia.[32p]
Phlegm accumulation leads to thick and sticky saliva. For this, half summer and southern star type herbs can be used; in severe cases, gourd calyx and zhiling can be considered. For stubborn accumulation causing stabbing pain in the sides, three-edged and broadleaf herbs can help; in worse cases, glycyrrhiza and scorpion's tail may be necessary. Water accumulation results in swollen legs and shins; for this, plum tree and commercial land can be used, with severe cases needing glycyrrhiza and candytuft. Blood accumulation manifests as bruises and stagnation, especially postpartum; herb peach pits and earth elm are suitable, with worse cases requiring wasp and leech. Flesh accumulation appears as lumps or tumors; for this, greasy powder and white lilac should be applied, possibly leading to bleeding through needle acupuncture; in severe circumstances, mine sand and trust stone might be used.

All nine types of accumulation are primarily based on qi, each needing targeted treatment according to their characteristics. Modern practitioners lump all these herbs together into one formula, claiming it can treat all accumulations – a grave error. Without the corresponding illness, why use this remedy? Without that condition, why employ that herb? With nine sheep herding ten goats, where should one turn? This approach not only lacks benefit but can also be harmful.

For unresolved accumulations, a remedy called "Wuyou San" can be taken, allowing for three to five bowel movements each month; one may also consider "Gui Ling Bai Zhu San" or "Miao Gong Wan." It is crucial to avoid consuming hard, sticky foods, wind-induced substances, and heating items.

Essentials of Healing Methods
(Yuan Dynasty, Zhang Jieg Ancient)

Cultivate the Right and Eliminate the Wrong[33p]
A robust person has no accumulation, but a weak person does. When the spleen and stomach are weak, and both qi and blood are deficient, one can feel the effects throughout the four seasons, which can lead to accumulation. If one hastily treats this with harsh medicines to break the accumulation, the disease may go, but the person's health will have already declined. Medicines like lacquer, sand, rush, rhubarb, and kalonia may provide temporary relief, but once the effect is gone, the problem persists. As qi diminishes and the disease worsens, what benefit is there? Therefore, when treating accumulation, one must first nourish the proper energies; then the accumulation will naturally resolve. For instance, if a gathering is composed entirely of gentlemen, even if there is one petty person, he will have no place to remain. As long as true qi is substantial and the gastric qi is strong, the accumulation will dissolve on its own. If there is substantial accumulation made of foul substances, one must be cautious with potent medicines; how much more so when dealing with a weak person with accumulation? This is one aspect of treating accumulation. The balance of evil and proper energies should be examined thoroughly.

Regarding clinical practice:

A distended mass in the center indicates phlegm and fluid; on the right, it indicates food accumulation; on the left, it indicates blood stasis. Qi cannot form a block or gather; a block is a tangible thing formed by phlegm, food accumulation, and stagnant blood. Use vinegar to boil sea stones, vinegar to boil rush, things like wormwood, peach kernels, safflower, myrrh, and cyperus to make pills, and swallow them with alkaline white atractylodes decoction. To treat the block, one must lower heat and disperse food accumulation, as food accumulation is also phlegm. To move stagnant blood, one must nourish significantly once the block is expelled. Accumulation disease should not be treated with purgatives, as they merely deplete the true qi, and the illness will not be resolved. Instead, one must use medicines that dissolve and melt the accumulation, thus eradicating the root cause. In general, if a woman has a mass, it is often due to blood stasis.[34p]
The accumulation in the five organs is called the five accumulations, while the accumulation in the six bowels is called the six collections. Accumulations have a definite shape, while collections have no fixed location. Regardless of which classic you refer to, it is advisable to take the Ten-Flavor Great Seven Qi Decoction and swallow the esteemed Red Pill.

In the "Classic of Needle Therapy" from the Yellow Emperor, it states: How does the intestine swell? Qi Bo replies: Cold qi lingers outside the intestines, clashing with the defensive qi, which cannot nourish properly. This leads to binding and internal attachment, causing bad qi to arise and polyps to develop. Initially, they are as large as a chicken egg, gradually growing larger until they resemble a child in the womb. If they persist for a long time, they’ll separate from the years, feeling solid when pressed and moving when pushed. They align with the menstrual cycle; this is its sign. The intestine refers to the large intestine, which is responsible for conduction and is associated with the lung. The lung governs the defensive qi, which is the vital energy; it disperses with warmth and solidifies with cold. Currently, cold lingers in the large intestine, causing the defensive qi to fail in nourishing, resulting in binding and the formation of stagnation internally. When this persists, it is called intestinal swelling. When qi disperses, it is clear; when qi collects, it is turbid. This stagnation leads to the rise of bad qi and the formation of polyps, gradually increasing in size until the abdomen swells, resembling that of a pregnant woman.[35p]
This condition arises from a deficiency in qi leading to a blood stagnation, hence the menstrual flow is irregular and does not occur as it should. This is not due to conception, and can be identified as such. Furthermore, when it comes to stone obstruction, Qi Bo said: stone obstruction occurs within the womb, caused by cold energy obstructing the uterine orifice. When the orifice is closed, it prevents the smooth flow of qi and causes stagnant blood that should flow out but does not, resulting in retention and growing larger over time, resembling pregnancy. The menstrual flow does not occur timely and these issues arise in women, and can be guided to flow downwards. The bladder serves as a reservoir for fluids; when qi transforms, it can be expelled. Now, if cold lodges in the uterine orifice, qi becomes blocked and stagnant, causing blood to accumulate and not flow. Retention becomes hardened like stone, which is known as stone obstruction. This illness begins as a qi condition followed by a blood condition, hence the absence of menstruation makes it possible to address and release the blockage. The "Nanjing" states: when deficient, it leads to internal stagnation, with men suffering from seven types of hernias and women experiencing obstructions. This is what is meant. Only potent herbal remedies can resolve it, such as the Jianxuan pill can be taken.[36p]
Formation of Food Accumulation

When a person's spleen and stomach are weak, whether due to excessive eating, frequent consumption of cold foods, or the inability to properly digest, it leads to the formation of accumulations and clumps, causing fullness in the heart and abdomen, sighing, sour regurgitation, a pale face, and a thin body.

There are ten types of accumulations:

  1. Food accumulation
  2. Alcohol accumulation
  3. Grain accumulation
  4. Meat accumulation
  5. Fish and crab accumulation
  6. Fruit and vegetable accumulation
  7. Tea accumulation
  8. Water accumulation
  9. Blood accumulation
  10. Worm accumulation

When food does not digest, leading to a feeling of heaviness and stagnation, it is advisable to use Ping Wei San with added Suan Zha, Xiang Fu, Shen Qu, Mai Ya, and Sheng Jiang. For accumulations resulting from alcohol, the face appears yellow and black, with occasional vomiting of phlegm and water; in such cases, Di Jin Yin Zi with added Ge Gen, Chi Fu Ling, Suan Zha, and Shen Qu is recommended.

If food is overconsumed, resulting in accumulation, use Lai Fu Zi Tang with added Ai Wei Yuan. For meat accumulation, Ai Wei Yuan is suggested. For excessive fish and crab consumption leading to accumulation, Xiang Su San with extra Sheng Jiang and Mu Xiang should be taken.

If fruit and vegetable accumulation occurs, Gui Xiang Wan is advised. For those who excessively consume tea or drink dry tea, Xing Zhu Wan, or the powdered form of gypsum, Huang Qin, and Sheng Ma mixed with sugar water is recommended.

For excessive drinking of water and juice leading to accumulation, with pulling pain in the chest and sides, and gurgling sounds, Shi Zao Tang should be used.

For blood stasis accumulation, either due to trauma or falling, causing it to settle in the chest and abdomen, resulting in a yellow face and dark stools, Di Dang Tang is recommended.

When food accumulations transform into worms, Miao Ying Wan is to be used.[37p]

Key Points on Diagnosis and Treatment by Dai Sigong (Ming Dynasty)

Discussion on Diagnosis and Treatment

If there is a drinking habit that forms a mass between the abdomen and ribs, resembling a form of accumulation, the use of breaking drugs is often ineffective. Instead, one should address the drinking issue and prescribe a phlegm-dispelling decoction. How to identify it as a drinking issue? The patient previously suffered from phlegm, experiencing excessive saliva, or has a longstanding phlegm condition.

For those with a drinking habit forming a drinking mass, resulting in abdominal bloating, pain, or even whole body swelling, along with yellowed skin and poor appetite, a decoction of ten ingredients known as the Great Seven Qi Decoction with red wine should be prepared and taken.

If there is an accumulation in the liver located beneath the left rib, shaped like an inverted cup or resembling a turtle, accompanied by nausea, pain in both sides, pulling sensation in the lower abdomen, cold feet, and cramping, over time, this can resemble malaria, termed "fat qi." The Great Seven Qi Decoction should be used; after cooking and cooling, take an iron implement, heat it until red-hot, and pour it over the medicine to be taken while hot.

For a lung accumulation situated beneath the right rib, comparable to an inverted cup, if there is reversed qi with back pain, shortness of breath with a tendency to forget, cold skin, and intermittent pain in the skin like being pricked by lice, over time, this can lead to coughing and wheezing, known as "xi bing." The Great Seven Qi Decoction should be administered, adding mulberry bark, pinellia, and apricot kernels, each at half a qian.[38p]
The heart accumulates below the navel, reaching to the heart, as large as an arm, with a feeling of heat in the abdomen, dryness in the throat, and irritability; in severe cases, there may be vomiting of blood, known as "伏梁" (Fú Liáng). It is suitable to use the Great Seven Qi Decoction with half a coin each of Stone Acorus and Pinellia.

The spleen accumulates in the stomach area, as large as an overturned cup, causing blockage, back pain, heart discomfort, reduced hunger and increased fullness, abdominal distention, vomiting, diarrhea, swollen feet, and loss of flesh. If prolonged, the extremities may become unresponsive, known as "痞气" (Pǐ Qì). It is best to use the Great Seven Qi Decoction with red round pills.

Kidney accumulation occurs in the lower abdomen, rushing upwards to the heart, without a fixed pattern, like a rushing piglet. There may be reduced hunger with increased fullness, urgent sensations in the lower abdomen, lower back pain, mouth dryness, dim vision, and cold bones. If prolonged, it may lead to bone weakness, known as "奔豚" (Bēn Tún). It is advisable to use the Great Seven Qi Decoction with double the amount of cinnamon and half a coin each of fennel and fried Melia fruit.

If there are sensations in the abdomen akin to a mass or distension, moving with the qi up and down, without a fixed location, it is appropriate to use the Scatter and Gather Decoction.

If there is pain caused by qi, wandering between the heart and abdomen, attacking upwards and downwards with a sound like thunder, it may be a formed mass or not; using a whole scorpion, crushed and boiled into a decoction, in combination with Suhexiang Pills is recommended.

If there is a proper accumulation site, internal heat like fire gradually affecting the limbs, occurring several times a day over two or three days before improvement, it is inappropriate to attack the heat.

There may also be original febrile illnesses, with heat remaining and not dissipating, leading to symptoms and masses; in this case, it is necessary to use heat-reducing agents concurrently.[39p]
Accumulation and Gathering

      Introduction to Medicine 【Ming Dynasty - Li Chen】

          All accumulations pertain to the Spleen

The classics state: Accumulation disorders, masses, and fullness are all due to the damp earth energy of the Taiyin. They initially arise from external influences or internal injuries causing qi stagnation. If treatment mistakenly supplements, it can lead to the formation of accumulation. Accumulation pertains to the yin energy dominating the five organs, with the pulse being deep and hidden. It may be found on either the left or the right, with roots felt, and pain has a consistent location.

Liver accumulation beneath the left ribs is called "fat qi," indicating an excess of wind qi causing stagnation in blood, leading to rib pain or intermittent fevers. Heart accumulation above the umbilicus is named "suspended beam," suggesting a beam-like obstruction beneath the heart that causes irritability, resulting from stifled fire. Hot medicine and moxibustion should be avoided.

Intestinal abscesses are similar; however, they present with swelling of the body, thighs, and back, with pain around the navel indicating an abscess. Spleen accumulation in the upper right abdomen is referred to as “distended qi,” wherein the yang qi is burdened by dampness, causing jaundice and fatigue, and food does not nourish the skin. Hot medicines are also to be avoided here.

Lung accumulation beneath the right ribs is called "breath obstruction," indicating rapid and upward flowing breath, leading to coughing and lung abscess. Kidney accumulation occurs in the lower abdomen, or beneath the heart is termed "running pig," evoking the image of a pig’s chaotic movement, causing alternating breath and bone atrophy, which is particularly difficult to treat.

Do not hastily induce vomiting or purging for any of these accumulations, as it merely depletes true qi without resolving the accumulation itself. Inducing vomiting for "running pig" is especially ill-advised.

There are five accumulations from ancient times, and today, the enhancement and adjustment of the five accumulation pills are even more refined.[40p]
The accumulation is formed by the Yang energy of the six bowels, with the pulse being submerged and knotty, sometimes hidden and sometimes visible, hair without roots, pain without a fixed place. Use Sanju Decoction, Qiqi Decoction, Da Awei Pill, Xiangling Pill, and Da An Pill with ginseng.

Accumulation initially manifests as cold, so it is suitable to use warming and dispersing methods with formulas like Great Qiqi Decoction and Black and White Pill. If it persists and becomes hot, it should be treated with warming and cooling methods such as Mukxiang Binglang Pill and Tongyuan Erba Dan. If there are parasites, use Miaoying Pill. For external treatment, use San Sheng Gao, Sanling Decoction, and Shenyang Awei Powder.

Moreover, Yang deficiency with accumulation is easier to treat, but Yin deficiency is difficult to remedy intensively. For phlegm accumulation, it is best to avoid stagnating medicines and to take nourishing medicines mixed with tortoise shell, turtle shell, autumn stone, and cinnabar. Take Zhilv Pill, Da An Pill, or Su Bie Pill at noon; these are good for dissolving and transforming.

If phlegm accumulation is accompanied by cold stagnation at the navel and is mistakenly thought to be chronic coldness, using ginger and attaching warming medicine may initially seem appropriate but can worsen phlegm accumulation over time, harming the true Qi and depleting Yin blood. However, substances like sulfur and Aconite must not be taken, just as Zhi, Ba, and Mendo cold and cool substances that harm the spleen and stagnate Qi are also unsuitable.

The ancients said: "Diminish it by more than half and then stop." They also said: "Nourishing the upright will naturally eliminate accumulation." This refers to cases of deficiency leading to accumulation. In addition to gradual tonification, one must also cut down on rich flavors, moderate desires, restrain violent anger, and maintain proper thoughts; this will help achieve overall health.[41p]

Masses

Phlegm masses can form into lumps from phlegm and food accumulation of stagnant blood, and these are categorized as accumulations. When the accumulation forms a lump that does not move, it is called a "symptom," indicating hardness and firmness. Those that may or may not be present, that can be found above or below, or to the left or right, are called "masses," indicating a shape formed from pseudo-blood that shows movement and has vitality.

For treatment on the left, one should focus on breaking the blood, using Hai Shi Wan or Dang Gui Long Hui Wan with five qian, adding one liang each of Tao Ren and Jiang Huang, then make into honey pills. For the right, one should regulate the qi using Qing Pi Tang or Mu Xiang Fen Qi Wan.

In cases of accumulation, use Xiao Jin Zuo Zhi San, Hong Wan Zi, Xiao A Wei Wan, or Dang Gui Long Hui Wan, and Bao He Wan, all with the addition of pigeon droppings.

For pathways of food and drink, emotional stress can lead to phlegm accumulation, treated with Shi Lian Wan or Bai Jie Wan. All masses beneath the skin membrane should be treated with Er Chen Tang, supplemented with qi tonics and qi-moving herbs.[42p]
Medical Canon Must-Read
(Ming Dynasty, Li Zhongzi)

General Principles of Treatment

When the accumulation of illness occurs, it is due to insufficient righteous qi allowing the malevolent qi to take hold, much like petty individuals in the court when the gentlemen weaken. Righteous qi and malevolent qi cannot coexist; when one rises, the other falls. As malevolent qi increases, righteous qi declines; if one does not attack it, loss will follow. However, if one attacks too hastily, righteous qi may be harmed. The three phases of treatment must be understood:

First, when the illness is newly arising, righteous qi is still strong and malevolent qi is shallow; it can be allowed to be attacked.

Second, when the illness has persisted longer, malevolent qi is deeper and righteous qi is weaker; one should both attack and supplement.

Third, when the illness has been prolonged, malevolent qi is pervasive and righteous qi has diminished; one should focus on supplementation.

This accumulation of illness represents a principle that builds up day by day, not something that can be resolved overnight; thus, the removal must also be gradual.[43p]
If one is too urgent, it harms the vital energy; if the vital energy is harmed, it cannot transform, and the evil spirits will persist. I have once formulated a remedy consisting of Yin and Yang accumulations, with slightly potent ingredients. When used in moderation, it replenishes for several days, then attacks; regardless of how much is removed, it is supplemented again. When the spirit is strong, then I attack again, repeatedly attacking and supplementing, aiming for balance. This is my unique method, always effective. The classics say: "A great accumulation and a great gathering can be violated, only diminish it by half." Therefore, by removing half of the accumulation, using pure, sweet, and warm nourishment to strengthen the spleen and earth, the residual accumulation will dissolve without force. If one insists on attacking without leaving any, the chances of causing death and calamity are few. The classics state: "Those who are strong have their energy flow smoothly and recover; the weak, when affected, become ill." The ancients said: "A strong person has no accumulation, while the weak may possess it; hence, one should nurture the upright to eliminate the evil." It is like a table full of gentlemen; no space will be available for one or two scoundrels.[44p]
Although this is merely the perspective of the inexperienced, if there is a significant accumulation and gathering, one should not merely search and chase after it. Daily application of supplements without understanding will not be beneficial. By carefully discerning what causes the illness and what contributes to the accumulation, once identified accurately, deploy the appropriate methods to address it—what concern would there be that it cannot be cured? The art of war states: a good attacker leaves the enemy unaware of their defenses; this is also akin to being a good strategist in medicine!

For strong pulses indicating a robust health, and weak ones indicating impending death. A pulse that is thin and deeply attached to the bones signifies an accumulation. A deep but strong pulse indicates a mass. A pulse that is deep and tight points to a cold accumulation. Conversely, a floating but firm pulse indicates a gathering.

For light accumulations of alcohol, one may use kudzu root, shenchu (a fermentation agent), huanglian (Coptis chinensis), and white cardamom; in more severe cases, one might employ gan sui (liquidambar) and qian niu zi (cowherb seed).

For light gas accumulations, one may consider using mu xiang (saussurea), zhi ke (bitter orange), hou po (magnolia bark), and orange peel; in more severe cases, one might use zhi shi (bitter orange fruit) and qian niu zi.[45p]
For light blood stasis: lacquer, peach kernel, peony, vincetoxicum, red peony, safflower; for severe cases: rhubarb, lizard, leech, pangolin, stone of flower bud.
For light phlegm accumulation: pinellia, (certain herb), for severe cases: phlegm pill. Old phlegm: sea stone, tile root. Phlegm in the skin and outer membranes: white mustard.
For light fluid retention: Wuling powder; for severe cases: zhuling, guansui, yanhua.
For light tea accumulation: turmeric, sesame; for severe cases: evodia, ginger.
For light food stagnation: trisacanthus, atriplex; for severe cases: ba shuang, rhubarb.
For light grain accumulation: malt, grain sprouts, shenqu, sand fruit; for severe cases: chicken inner gold.
For light meat accumulation: hawthorn, ferula; for severe cases: ningsha, nitrate.
For egg accumulation: white cardamom, tangerine peel, fermented soybeans, ginger juice.
For vegetable accumulation: clove, cinnamon, musk.
For flatulence: radish seeds, ginger wine decoction.[46p]
Fish and turtle accumulation can be treated with perilla leaves, orange peel, wood incense, and ginger juice. Turtle accumulation is treated with white horse urine. Dog meat accumulation can be treated with almonds and hawthorn.

Insect accumulation is treated with realgar, tin ash, areca nut, thunderbolt pills, wild yam, nut pine, and the Sage gentleman. Malaria accumulation is treated with turtle shell and grass fruit.

Discussion

Diseases of accumulation result from food, blood, Qi, wind, and cold. However, the terms "accumulation" and "gathering" need to be distinguished. Accumulation refers to what has built up gradually over time. Gathering refers to what collects and disperses intermittently. An accumulation is solid and unchanging; thus, it is termed "accumulation" (积) because it has form. In contrast, what gathers and disperses, lacking a fixed form, is called "gathering" (聚).

All tangible conditions may arise from either obstruction in food and drink or the retention of pus and blood—when fluids congeal, they lead to symptomatic masses, all categorized as accumulations, primarily affecting the blood, which is tangible and static.

Conversely, intangible conditions, whether distended or not, painful or not, arising sporadically and transiently, classify as gatherings—a category primarily concerning Qi, which is intangible and dynamic. Hence, the "Nan Jing" designates accumulation as yin Qi and gathering as yang Qi, which expresses this very meaning.

Therefore, for any intangible gathering, dispersal is easy; for tangible accumulations, dissolution is challenging. When diagnosing these conditions, one should distinguish between the tangible and intangible, whether related to Qi or blood, and the treatment for accumulation and gathering will naturally become clearer.[47p]
The accumulation from food and drink, when it momentarily accumulates, is merely due to dietary injuries, which must occur within the intestines and stomach; thus, it can be expelled or alleviated, making treatment not difficult. However, if one's eating habits lack moderation, leading to gradual retention, there may be a feeling of fullness in the left side of the diaphragm, because of the stomach's extensive connections known as "empty within," which emerges beneath the left breast and is responsive to clothing; this is the pathway of the Yangming Qi. If one experiences irregular hunger and fullness, with food being consumed successively, this can cause the Yangming stomach Qi to become disturbed. If it encounters cold Yin energy, the spleen is unable to transform it, resulting in lingering accumulation that combines with the fluids outside the intestines, clumping together and becoming difficult to disperse, eventually forming a condition of accumulation. Initially, this is very slight and many people do not notice it; as it persists, it takes root and becomes firmly established, making medicinal treatment difficult. Currently, many children in the northwest suffer from this condition, especially in regions where wheat is a staple; this is precisely because wheat is prone to stagnation, leaving disease beneath the skin and membranes, making it hard to treat. Similarly, women who experience blood disorders and Qi stagnation, whether above or below, often have issues outside the intestines and stomach, between the sources of the innate essence; therefore, one should gradually seek methods to dissolve it. Care must be taken not to act recklessly in haste, as doing so may hurt the stomach Qi, causing the accumulation to remain, which would only hasten the danger. Additionally, external influences of wind and cold can also lead to accumulation.[48p]
As the classic text states: The empty evil is within, lingering and not departing, residing outside the intestines and stomach, gathering between the origins, remaining within the meridians, breathing and forming accumulations. It also says: The illness is named "hidden beam," and this is the root of the wind. From this perspective, we see that people nowadays who develop a mass after contracting malaria, this is indeed related to the wind and cold, and can be inferred. However, while malaria is clearly related to wind and cold, the accumulations due to wind seem unrelated. It is unknown that the stagnation of food does not necessarily lead to accumulation just due to cold, and the evil of wind and cold does not necessarily take shape without food. Therefore, it is essential for food to encounter cold, and for cold to encounter food, otherwise, when the superficial evil has not been cleared, excessive food intake will hinder it, leading to accumulation. The text states: The empty evil wind interacts with the body's form, and the two vacuities come together, thus lodging in the form. Truly, the origin of accumulation is often due to this. Even blood symptoms and qi stagnation arise from this. However, when accumulations are retained by cold, if retained for long, cold often turns to heat; when wind causes accumulation, and the accumulation forms, it is no longer wind-related. Thus, those treating this should only focus on the area where it is retained, and must not disperse it, so as not to further injure the vital energy. Only careful patients can understand the origin and reflect on it at the onset; then they can formulate effective strategies to preserve the spleen.[49p]
The accumulation of symptoms can manifest anywhere, whether above or below, left or right, as there is no fixed location. Generally, blood accumulates more in the lower parts, while qi and food accumulations are situated above the stomach and below in the lower abdomen. Any stagnation can potentially settle anywhere. I once treated a patient with food-related pain located at the lower right corner of the abdomen.
